<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Star</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the astronomical object. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Star_(disambiguation)" title="Star (disambiguation)">Star (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Starsinthesky.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2482" data-file-width="3877" decoding="async" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Starsinthesky.jpg/220px-Starsinthesky.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Starsinthesky.jpg/330px-Starsinthesky.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Starsinthesky.jpg/440px-Starsinthesky.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Starsinthesky.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Star_formation#Stellar_nurseries" title="Star formation">star-forming</a> region in the <a href="/wiki/Large_Magellanic_Cloud" title="Large Magellanic Cloud">Large Magellanic Cloud</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Sun_by_the_Atmospheric_Imaging_Assembly_of_NASA%27s_Solar_Dynamics_Observatory_-_20100819.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3860" data-file-width="4044" decoding="async" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/The_Sun_by_the_Atmospheric_Imaging_Assembly_of_NASA%27s_Solar_Dynamics_Observatory_-_20100819.jpg/220px-The_Sun_by_the_Atmospheric_Imaging_Assembly_of_NASA%27s_Solar_Dynamics_Observatory_-_20100819.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/The_Sun_by_the_Atmospheric_Imaging_Assembly_of_NASA%27s_Solar_Dynamics_Observatory_-_20100819.jpg/330px-The_Sun_by_the_Atmospheric_Imaging_Assembly_of_NASA%27s_Solar_Dynamics_Observatory_-_20100819.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/The_Sun_by_the_Atmospheric_Imaging_Assembly_of_NASA%27s_Solar_Dynamics_Observatory_-_20100819.jpg/440px-The_Sun_by_the_Atmospheric_Imaging_Assembly_of_NASA%27s_Solar_Dynamics_Observatory_-_20100819.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Sun_by_the_Atmospheric_Imaging_Assembly_of_NASA%27s_Solar_Dynamics_Observatory_-_20100819.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>False-color imagery of the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>, a <a href="/wiki/G-type_main-sequence_star" title="G-type main-sequence star">G-type main-sequence star</a>, the closest to Earth</div></div></div>
<p>A <b>star</b> is type of <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_object" title="Astronomical object">astronomical object</a> consisting of a luminous <a href="/wiki/Spheroid" title="Spheroid">spheroid</a> of <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasma</a> held together by its own <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a>. The nearest star to <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>. Many other stars are visible to the naked eye from Earth during the night, appearing as a multitude of <a href="/wiki/Fixed_stars" title="Fixed stars">fixed</a> luminous points in the sky due to their immense distance from Earth. Historically, the most prominent stars were grouped into <a href="/wiki/Constellation" title="Constellation">constellations</a> and <a href="/wiki/Asterism_(astronomy)" title="Asterism (astronomy)">asterisms</a>, the brightest of which gained proper names. Astronomers have assembled <a href="/wiki/Star_catalogue" title="Star catalogue">star catalogues</a> that identify the known stars and provide standardized <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stellar_designation" title="Stellar designation">stellar designations</a>. However, most of the estimated 300 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sextillion" title="Sextillion">sextillion</a> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7023300000000000000♠</span>3<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>23</sup></span>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borenstein2010_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Borenstein2010-1">[1]</a></sup> stars in the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">Universe</a>, including all stars outside our <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">galaxy</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a>, are invisible to the naked eye from Earth.
</p><p>For at least a portion of its life, a star shines due to <a href="/wiki/Thermonuclear_fusion" title="Thermonuclear fusion">thermonuclear fusion</a> of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> into <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> in its core, releasing energy that traverses the star's interior and then <a href="/wiki/Radiation" title="Radiation">radiates</a> into <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">outer space</a>. Almost all naturally occurring elements heavier than helium are created by <a href="/wiki/Stellar_nucleosynthesis" title="Stellar nucleosynthesis">stellar nucleosynthesis</a> during the star's lifetime, and for some stars by <a href="/wiki/Supernova_nucleosynthesis" title="Supernova nucleosynthesis">supernova nucleosynthesis</a> when it explodes. Near the end of its life, a star can also contain <a href="/wiki/Degenerate_matter" title="Degenerate matter">degenerate matter</a>. <a href="/wiki/Astronomer" title="Astronomer">Astronomers</a> can determine the <a href="/wiki/Stellar_mass" title="Stellar mass">mass</a>, age, <a href="/wiki/Metallicity" title="Metallicity">metallicity</a> (chemical composition), and many other properties of a star by observing its motion through space, its <a href="/wiki/Luminosity" title="Luminosity">luminosity</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_spectroscopy" title="Astronomical spectroscopy">spectrum</a> respectively. The total mass of a star is the main factor that determines its <a href="/wiki/Stellar_evolution" title="Stellar evolution">evolution</a> and eventual fate. Other characteristics of a star, including diameter and temperature, change over its life, while the star's environment affects its rotation and movement. A plot of the temperature of many stars against their luminosities produces a plot known as a <a href="/wiki/Hertzsprung%E2%80%93Russell_diagram" title="Hertzsprung–Russell diagram">Hertzsprung–Russell diagram</a> (H–R diagram). Plotting a particular star on that diagram allows the age and evolutionary state of that star to be determined.
</p><p>A star's life <a href="/wiki/Star_formation" title="Star formation">begins</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_collapse" title="Gravitational collapse">gravitational collapse</a> of a gaseous <a href="/wiki/Nebula" title="Nebula">nebula</a> of material composed primarily of hydrogen, along with helium and trace amounts of heavier elements. When the stellar core is sufficiently dense, hydrogen becomes steadily converted into helium through nuclear fusion, releasing energy in the process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sunshine_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-sunshine-2">[2]</a></sup> The remainder of the star's interior carries energy away from the core through a combination of <a href="/wiki/Radiation" title="Radiation">radiative</a> and <a href="/wiki/Convection" title="Convection">convective</a> <a href="/wiki/Heat_transfer" title="Heat transfer">heat transfer</a> processes. The star's internal pressure prevents it from collapsing further under its own gravity. A star with mass greater than 0.4 times the Sun's will expand to become a <a href="/wiki/Red_giant" title="Red giant">red giant</a> when the hydrogen <a href="/wiki/Fuel" title="Fuel">fuel</a> in its core is exhausted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-late_stages_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-late_stages-3">[3]</a></sup>  In some cases, it will fuse heavier <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">elements</a> at the core or in shells around the core. As the star expands it throws a part of its mass, enriched with those heavier elements, into the interstellar environment, to be recycled later as new stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Meanwhile, the core becomes a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stellar_remnant" title="Stellar remnant">stellar remnant</a>: a <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">white dwarf</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star" title="Neutron star">neutron star</a>, or if it is sufficiently massive a <a href="/wiki/Black_hole" title="Black hole">black hole</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Binary_star" title="Binary star">Binary</a> and multi-star systems consist of two or more stars that are gravitationally bound and generally move around each other in stable <a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">orbits</a>. When two such stars have a relatively close orbit, their gravitational interaction can have a significant impact on their evolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iben_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-iben-5">[5]</a></sup> Stars can form part of a much larger gravitationally bound structure, such as a <a href="/wiki/Star_cluster" title="Star cluster">star cluster</a> or a galaxy.
</p>
