<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Observation_history">Observation history</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dibuix_de_Leo.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="292" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Dibuix_de_Leo.png/220px-Dibuix_de_Leo.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Dibuix_de_Leo.png/330px-Dibuix_de_Leo.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0f/Dibuix_de_Leo.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dibuix_de_Leo.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>People have seen patterns in the stars since ancient times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-forbes_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-forbes-6">[6]</a></sup> This 1690 depiction of the constellation of <a href="/wiki/Leo_(constellation)" title="Leo (constellation)">Leo</a>, the lion, is by <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Hevelius" title="Johannes Hevelius">Johannes Hevelius</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LeoCC.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="342" decoding="async" height="329" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/LeoCC.jpg/220px-LeoCC.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/LeoCC.jpg/330px-LeoCC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a8/LeoCC.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LeoCC.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The constellation of <a href="/wiki/Leo_(constellation)" title="Leo (constellation)">Leo</a> as it can be seen by the naked eye. Lines have been added.</div></div></div>
<p>Historically, stars have been important to <a href="/wiki/Civilization" title="Civilization">civilizations</a> throughout the world. They have been part of religious practices and used for <a href="/wiki/Celestial_navigation" title="Celestial navigation">celestial navigation</a> and orientation. Many ancient astronomers believed that stars were permanently affixed to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heavenly_sphere" title="Heavenly sphere">heavenly sphere</a> and that they were immutable. By convention, astronomers grouped stars into <a href="/wiki/Constellation" title="Constellation">constellations</a> and used them to track the motions of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planets" title="Planets">planets</a> and the inferred position of the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-forbes_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-forbes-6">[6]</a></sup> The motion of the Sun against the background stars (and the horizon) was used to create <a href="/wiki/Solar_calendar" title="Solar calendar">calendars</a>, which could be used to regulate agricultural practices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_calendar" title="Gregorian calendar">Gregorian calendar</a>, currently used nearly everywhere in the world, is a <a href="/wiki/Solar_calendar" title="Solar calendar">solar calendar</a> based on the angle of the Earth's rotational axis relative to its local star, the Sun.
</p><p>The oldest accurately dated <a href="/wiki/Star_chart" title="Star chart">star chart</a> was the result of ancient <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_astronomy" title="Egyptian astronomy">Egyptian astronomy</a> in 1534 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Babylonian_star_catalogues" title="Babylonian star catalogues">earliest known star catalogues</a> were compiled by the ancient <a href="/wiki/Babylonian_astronomy" title="Babylonian astronomy">Babylonian astronomers</a> of <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> in the late 2nd millennium BC, during the <a href="/wiki/Kassites" title="Kassites">Kassite Period</a> (c. 1531–1155 BC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-north_1995_30_31_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-north_1995_30_31-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first <a href="/wiki/Star_catalogue" title="Star catalogue">star catalogue</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_astronomy" title="Ancient Greek astronomy">Greek astronomy</a> was created by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aristillus" title="Aristillus">Aristillus</a> in approximately 300 BC, with the help of <a href="/wiki/Timocharis" title="Timocharis">Timocharis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> The star catalog of <a href="/wiki/Hipparchus" title="Hipparchus">Hipparchus</a> (2nd century BC) included 1020 stars, and was used to assemble <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a>'s star catalogue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Hipparchus is known for the discovery of the first recorded <i><a href="/wiki/Nova" title="Nova">nova</a></i> (new star).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Many of the constellations and star names in use today derive from Greek astronomy.
</p><p>In spite of the apparent immutability of the heavens, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_astronomy" title="Chinese astronomy">Chinese astronomers</a> were aware that new stars could appear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clark_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-clark-14">[14]</a></sup> In 185 AD, they were the first to observe and write about a <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernova</a>, now known as the <a href="/wiki/SN_185" title="SN 185">SN 185</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> The brightest stellar event in recorded history was the <a href="/wiki/SN_1006" title="SN 1006">SN 1006</a> supernova, which was observed in 1006 and written about by the Egyptian astronomer <a href="/wiki/Ali_ibn_Ridwan" title="Ali ibn Ridwan">Ali ibn Ridwan</a> and several Chinese astronomers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/SN_1054" title="SN 1054">SN 1054</a> supernova, which gave birth to the <a href="/wiki/Crab_Nebula" title="Crab Nebula">Crab Nebula</a>, was also observed by Chinese and Islamic astronomers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SN1054_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-SN1054-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PASP1942_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-PASP1942-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronomy_in_medieval_Islam" title="Astronomy in medieval Islam">Medieval Islamic astronomers</a> gave <a href="/wiki/List_of_Arabic_star_names" title="List of Arabic star names">Arabic names to many stars</a> that are still used today and they invented numerous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronomy_in_medieval_Islam#Instruments" title="Astronomy in medieval Islam">astronomical instruments</a> that could compute the positions of the stars. They built the first large <a href="/wiki/Observatory" title="Observatory">observatory</a> research institutes, mainly for the purpose of producing <i><a href="/wiki/Zij" title="Zij">Zij</a></i> star catalogues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Among these, the <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Fixed_Stars" title="Book of Fixed Stars">Book of Fixed Stars</a></i> (964) was written by the <a href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">Persian</a> astronomer <a href="/wiki/Abd_al-Rahman_al-Sufi" title="Abd al-Rahman al-Sufi">Abd al-Rahman al-Sufi</a>, who observed a number of stars, <a href="/wiki/Star_cluster" title="Star cluster">star clusters</a> (including the <a href="/wiki/Omicron_Velorum" title="Omicron Velorum">Omicron Velorum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brocchi%27s_Cluster" title="Brocchi's Cluster">Brocchi's Clusters</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">galaxies</a> (including the <a href="/wiki/Andromeda_Galaxy" title="Andromeda Galaxy">Andromeda Galaxy</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jones_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jones-21">[21]</a></sup> According to A. Zahoor, in the 11th century, the Persian <a href="/wiki/Polymath" title="Polymath">polymath</a> scholar <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abu_Rayhan_Biruni" title="Abu Rayhan Biruni">Abu Rayhan Biruni</a> described the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a> galaxy as a multitude of fragments having the properties of <a href="/wiki/Nebula" title="Nebula">nebulous</a> stars, and also gave the <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitudes</a> of various stars during a <a href="/wiki/Lunar_eclipse" title="Lunar eclipse">lunar eclipse</a> in 1019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Josep Puig, the <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Andalusian</a> astronomer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ibn_Bajjah" title="Ibn Bajjah">Ibn Bajjah</a> proposed that the Milky Way was made up of many stars that almost touched one another and appeared to be a continuous image due to the effect of <a href="/wiki/Refraction" title="Refraction">refraction</a> from sublunary material, citing his observation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conjunction_(astronomy_and_astrology)" title="Conjunction (astronomy and astrology)">conjunction</a> of Jupiter and Mars on 500 <a href="/wiki/Islamic_calendar" title="Islamic calendar">AH</a> (1106/1107 AD) as evidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Montada_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Montada-23">[23]</a></sup> 
Early European astronomers such as <a href="/wiki/Tycho_Brahe" title="Tycho Brahe">Tycho Brahe</a> identified new stars in the <a href="/wiki/Night_sky" title="Night sky">night sky</a> (later termed <i>novae</i>), suggesting that the heavens were not immutable. In 1584, <a href="/wiki/Giordano_Bruno" title="Giordano Bruno">Giordano Bruno</a> suggested that the stars were like the Sun, and may have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extrasolar_planet" title="Extrasolar planet">other planets</a>, possibly even Earth-like, in orbit around them,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-he_history_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-he_history-24">[24]</a></sup> an idea that had been suggested earlier by the ancient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_philosophy" title="Greek philosophy">Greek philosophers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Democritus" title="Democritus">Democritus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Epicurus" title="Epicurus">Epicurus</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> and by medieval <a href="/wiki/Cosmology_in_medieval_Islam" title="Cosmology in medieval Islam">Islamic cosmologists</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> such as <a href="/wiki/Fakhr_al-Din_al-Razi" title="Fakhr al-Din al-Razi">Fakhr al-Din al-Razi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Setia_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Setia-27">[27]</a></sup> By the following century, the idea of the stars being the same as the Sun was reaching a consensus among astronomers. To explain why these stars exerted no net gravitational pull on the Solar System, <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a> suggested that the stars were equally distributed in every direction, an idea prompted by the theologian <a href="/wiki/Richard_Bentley" title="Richard Bentley">Richard Bentley</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Italian astronomer <a href="/wiki/Geminiano_Montanari" title="Geminiano Montanari">Geminiano Montanari</a> recorded observing variations in luminosity of the star <a href="/wiki/Algol" title="Algol">Algol</a> in 1667. <a href="/wiki/Edmond_Halley" title="Edmond Halley">Edmond Halley</a> published the first measurements of the <a href="/wiki/Proper_motion" title="Proper motion">proper motion</a> of a pair of nearby "fixed" stars, demonstrating that they had changed positions since the time of the ancient <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greek</a> astronomers Ptolemy and Hipparchus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-he_history_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-he_history-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/William_Herschel" title="William Herschel">William Herschel</a> was the first astronomer to attempt to determine the distribution of stars in the sky. During the 1780s, he established a series of gauges in 600 directions and counted the stars observed along each line of sight. From this he deduced that the number of stars steadily increased toward one side of the sky, in the direction of the Milky Way <a href="/wiki/Galactic_Center" title="Galactic Center">core</a>. His son <a href="/wiki/John_Herschel" title="John Herschel">John Herschel</a> repeated this study in the southern hemisphere and found a corresponding increase in the same direction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> In addition to his other accomplishments, William Herschel is also noted for his discovery that some stars do not merely lie along the same line of sight, but are also physical companions that form binary star systems.
</p><p>The science of <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_spectroscopy" title="Astronomical spectroscopy">stellar spectroscopy</a> was pioneered by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_von_Fraunhofer" title="Joseph von Fraunhofer">Joseph von Fraunhofer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Angelo_Secchi" title="Angelo Secchi">Angelo Secchi</a>. By comparing the spectra of stars such as <a href="/wiki/Sirius" title="Sirius">Sirius</a> to the Sun, they found differences in the strength and number of their <a href="/wiki/Spectral_line" title="Spectral line">absorption lines</a>—the dark lines in stellar spectra caused by the atmosphere's absorption of specific frequencies. In 1865, Secchi began classifying stars into <a href="/wiki/Stellar_classification" title="Stellar classification">spectral types</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> However, the modern version of the stellar classification scheme was developed by <a href="/wiki/Annie_Jump_Cannon" title="Annie Jump Cannon">Annie J. Cannon</a> during the 1900s.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alpha_Centauri_AB_over_limb_of_Saturn_PIA10406.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="477" data-file-width="1017" decoding="async" height="103" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Alpha_Centauri_AB_over_limb_of_Saturn_PIA10406.jpg/220px-Alpha_Centauri_AB_over_limb_of_Saturn_PIA10406.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Alpha_Centauri_AB_over_limb_of_Saturn_PIA10406.jpg/330px-Alpha_Centauri_AB_over_limb_of_Saturn_PIA10406.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Alpha_Centauri_AB_over_limb_of_Saturn_PIA10406.jpg/440px-Alpha_Centauri_AB_over_limb_of_Saturn_PIA10406.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alpha_Centauri_AB_over_limb_of_Saturn_PIA10406.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Alpha_Centauri" title="Alpha Centauri">Alpha Centauri A</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alpha_Centauri_B" title="Alpha Centauri B">B</a> over limb of <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a></div></div></div>
<p>The first direct measurement of the distance to a star (<a href="/wiki/61_Cygni" title="61 Cygni">61 Cygni</a> at 11.4 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light-years" title="Light-years">light-years</a>) was made in 1838 by <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Bessel" title="Friedrich Bessel">Friedrich Bessel</a> using the <a href="/wiki/Parallax" title="Parallax">parallax</a> technique. Parallax measurements demonstrated the vast separation of the stars in the heavens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-he_history_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-he_history-24">[24]</a></sup> Observation of double stars gained increasing importance during the 19th century. In 1834, Friedrich Bessel observed changes in the proper motion of the star Sirius and inferred a hidden companion. <a href="/wiki/Edward_Charles_Pickering" title="Edward Charles Pickering">Edward Pickering</a> discovered the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spectroscopic_binary" title="Spectroscopic binary">spectroscopic binary</a> in 1899 when he observed the periodic splitting of the spectral lines of the star <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mizar_(star)" title="Mizar (star)">Mizar</a> in a 104-day period. Detailed observations of many binary star systems were collected by astronomers such as <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Georg_Wilhelm_von_Struve" title="Friedrich Georg Wilhelm von Struve">Friedrich Georg Wilhelm von Struve</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sherburne_Wesley_Burnham" title="Sherburne Wesley Burnham">S. W. Burnham</a>, allowing the masses of stars to be determined from computation of <a href="/wiki/Orbital_elements" title="Orbital elements">orbital elements</a>. The first solution to the problem of deriving an orbit of binary stars from telescope observations was made by Felix Savary in 1827.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
The twentieth century saw increasingly rapid advances in the scientific study of stars. The <a href="/wiki/Photograph" title="Photograph">photograph</a> became a valuable astronomical tool. <a href="/wiki/Karl_Schwarzschild" title="Karl Schwarzschild">Karl Schwarzschild</a> discovered that the color of a star and, hence, its temperature, could be determined by comparing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visual_magnitude" title="Visual magnitude">visual magnitude</a> against the <a href="/wiki/Photographic_magnitude" title="Photographic magnitude">photographic magnitude</a>. The development of the <a href="/wiki/Photoelectric_effect" title="Photoelectric effect">photoelectric</a> <a href="/wiki/Photometer" title="Photometer">photometer</a> allowed precise measurements of magnitude at multiple wavelength intervals. In 1921 <a href="/wiki/Albert_A._Michelson" title="Albert A. Michelson">Albert A. Michelson</a> made the first measurements of a stellar diameter using an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interferometer" title="Interferometer">interferometer</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Mount_Wilson_Observatory#Hooker_telescope" title="Mount Wilson Observatory">Hooker telescope</a> at <a href="/wiki/Mount_Wilson_Observatory" title="Mount Wilson Observatory">Mount Wilson Observatory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>Important theoretical work on the physical structure of stars occurred during the first decades of the twentieth century. In 1913, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hertzsprung-Russell_diagram" title="Hertzsprung-Russell diagram">Hertzsprung-Russell diagram</a> was developed, propelling the astrophysical study of stars. Successful <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stellar_model" title="Stellar model">models</a> were developed to explain the interiors of stars and stellar evolution. <a href="/wiki/Cecilia_Payne-Gaposchkin" title="Cecilia Payne-Gaposchkin">Cecilia Payne-Gaposchkin</a> first proposed that stars were made primarily of hydrogen and helium in her 1925 PhD thesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> The spectra of stars were further understood through advances in <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum physics</a>. This allowed the chemical composition of the stellar atmosphere to be determined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-new_cosmos_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-new_cosmos-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Milky_Way_IR_Spitzer.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2525" data-file-width="3501" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Milky_Way_IR_Spitzer.jpg/220px-Milky_Way_IR_Spitzer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Milky_Way_IR_Spitzer.jpg/330px-Milky_Way_IR_Spitzer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Milky_Way_IR_Spitzer.jpg/440px-Milky_Way_IR_Spitzer.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Milky_Way_IR_Spitzer.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The infrared image from NASA's <a href="/wiki/Spitzer_Space_Telescope" title="Spitzer Space Telescope">Spitzer Space Telescope</a> shows hundreds of thousands of stars in the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a> galaxy</div></div></div>
<p>With the exception of supernovae, individual stars have primarily been observed in the <a href="/wiki/Local_Group" title="Local Group">Local Group</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> and especially in the visible part of the Milky Way (as demonstrated by the detailed star catalogues available for our
galaxy).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> But some stars have been observed in the M100 galaxy of the <a href="/wiki/Virgo_Cluster" title="Virgo Cluster">Virgo Cluster</a>, about 100 million light years from the Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Local_Supercluster" title="Local Supercluster">Local Supercluster</a> it is possible to see star clusters, and current telescopes could in principle observe faint individual stars in the Local Group<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cepheids" title="Cepheids">Cepheids</a>). However, outside the Local Supercluster of galaxies, neither individual stars nor clusters of stars have been observed. The only exception is a faint image of a large star cluster containing hundreds of thousands of stars located at a distance of one billion light years<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>—ten times further than the most distant star cluster previously observed.
</p><p>In February 2018, astronomers reported, for the first time, a signal of the <a href="/wiki/Reionization" title="Reionization">reionization</a> epoch, an indirect detection of light from the earliest stars formed—about 180 million years after the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAT-20180228_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-20180228-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>In April, 2018, astronomers reported the detection of the most distant "ordinary" (i.e., <a href="/wiki/Main_sequence" title="Main sequence">main sequence</a>) star, named <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Icarus_(star)" title="Icarus (star)">Icarus</a> (formally, <a href="/wiki/MACS_J1149_Lensed_Star_1" title="MACS J1149 Lensed Star 1">MACS J1149 Lensed Star 1</a>), at 9 billion light-years away from <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NA-20180402_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-NA-20180402-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SPC-20180402_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-SPC-20180402-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>In May 2018, astronomers reported the detection of the most distant oxygen ever detected in the Universe—and the most distant galaxy ever observed by <a href="/wiki/Atacama_Large_Millimeter_Array" title="Atacama Large Millimeter Array">Atacama Large Millimeter Array</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Very_Large_Telescope" title="Very Large Telescope">Very Large Telescope</a>—with the team inferring that the signal was emitted 13.3 billion years ago (or 500 million years after the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a>. They found that the observed brightness of the galaxy is well-explained by a model where the onset of star formation corresponds to only 250 million years after the Universe began, corresponding to a <a href="/wiki/Redshift" title="Redshift">redshift</a> of about 15.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Designations">Designations</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stellar_designation" title="Stellar designation">Stellar designation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_naming_conventions" title="Astronomical naming conventions">Astronomical naming conventions</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Star_catalogue" title="Star catalogue">Star catalogue</a></div>
<p>The concept of a constellation was known to exist during the <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylonian</a> period. Ancient sky watchers imagined that prominent arrangements of stars formed patterns, and they associated these with particular aspects of nature or their myths. Twelve of these formations lay along the band of the <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic" title="Ecliptic">ecliptic</a> and these became the basis of <a href="/wiki/Astrology" title="Astrology">astrology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-koch95_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-koch95-44">[44]</a></sup> Many of the more prominent individual stars were also given names, particularly with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab_language" title="Arab language">Arabic</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a> designations.
</p><p>As well as certain constellations and the Sun itself, individual stars have their own <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mythology" title="Mythology">myths</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mythology_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-mythology-45">[45]</a></sup> To the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_religion" title="Ancient Greek religion">Ancient Greeks</a>, some "stars", known as <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planets</a> (Greek πλανήτης (planētēs), meaning "wanderer"), represented various important deities, from which the names of the planets <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a>, <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a> were taken.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mythology_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-mythology-45">[45]</a></sup> (<a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a> were also <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roman_mythology" title="Roman mythology">Roman gods</a>, but neither planet was known in Antiquity because of their low brightness. Their names were assigned by later astronomers.)
</p><p>Circa 1600, the names of the constellations were used to name the stars in the corresponding regions of the sky. The German astronomer <a href="/wiki/Johann_Bayer" title="Johann Bayer">Johann Bayer</a> created a series of star maps and applied Greek letters as <a href="/wiki/Bayer_designation" title="Bayer designation">designations</a> to the stars in each constellation. Later a numbering system based on the star's <a href="/wiki/Right_ascension" title="Right ascension">right ascension</a> was invented and added to <a href="/wiki/John_Flamsteed" title="John Flamsteed">John Flamsteed</a>'s star catalogue in his book <i>"Historia coelestis Britannica"</i> (the 1712 edition), whereby this numbering system came to be called <i><a href="/wiki/Flamsteed_designation" title="Flamsteed designation">Flamsteed designation</a></i> or <i>Flamsteed numbering</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>The only internationally recognized authority for naming celestial bodies is the <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">International Astronomical Union</a> (IAU).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-space_law09_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-space_law09-48">[48]</a></sup> The International Astronomical Union maintains the <a href="/wiki/IAU_Working_Group_on_Star_Names" title="IAU Working Group on Star Names">Working Group on Star Names</a> (WGSN)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WGSN_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-WGSN-49">[49]</a></sup> which catalogs and standardizes proper names for stars. A number of private companies sell names of stars, which the <a href="/wiki/British_Library" title="British Library">British Library</a> calls an <a href="/wiki/Regulation" title="Regulation">unregulated</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commercial_enterprise" title="Commercial enterprise">commercial enterprise</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astrometry05_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-astrometry05-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bl_disclaimer_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-bl_disclaimer-51">[51]</a></sup> The IAU has disassociated itself from this commercial practice, and these names are neither recognized by the IAU, professional astronomers, nor the amateur astronomy community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-andersen10_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-andersen10-52">[52]</a></sup> One such star-naming company is the <a href="/wiki/International_Star_Registry" title="International Star Registry">International Star Registry</a>, which, during the 1980s, was accused of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deceptive_practice" title="Deceptive practice">deceptive practice</a> for making it appear that the assigned name was <a href="/wiki/Official#Adjective" title="Official">official</a>. This now-discontinued ISR practice was informally labeled a scam and a fraud,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-si30_5_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-si30_5-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sd19980401_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-sd19980401-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-golden_faflick82_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-golden_faflick82-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-di_justo20011226_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-di_justo20011226-56">[56]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_Department_of_Consumer_Affairs" title="New York City Department of Consumer Affairs">New York City Department of Consumer Affairs</a> issued a violation against ISR for engaging in a deceptive trade practice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pliat02_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-pliat02-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sclafani19980508_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-sclafani19980508-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Units_of_measurement">Units of measurement</span></h2>
<p>Although stellar parameters can be expressed in <a href="/wiki/International_System_of_Units" title="International System of Units">SI units</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CGS_unit" title="CGS unit">CGS units</a>, it is often most convenient to express <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Luminosity" title="Luminosity">luminosity</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Radius" title="Radius">radii</a> in solar units, based on the characteristics of the Sun. In 2015, the IAU defined a set of <i>nominal</i> solar values (defined as SI constants, without uncertainties) which can be used for quoting stellar parameters:
</p>
<dl><dd><table>
<tbody><tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Solar_luminosity" title="Solar luminosity">nominal solar luminosity</a>:
</td>
<td><i>L</i><sub>⊙</sub> = <span class="nowrap">3.828 × 10<sup>26</sup> W</span> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prsa16_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Prsa16-59">[59]</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Solar_radius" title="Solar radius">nominal solar radius</a>
</td>
<td><i>R</i><sub>⊙</sub> = <span class="nowrap">6.957 × 10<sup>8</sup> m</span> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prsa16_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-Prsa16-59">[59]</a></sup>
</td></tr></tbody></table></dd></dl>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Solar_mass" title="Solar mass">solar mass</a> M<sub>⊙</sub> was not explicitly defined by the IAU due to the large relative uncertainty (10<sup>−4</sup>) of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_Constant" title="Gravitational Constant">Newtonian gravitational constant</a> G. However, since the product of the Newtonian gravitational constant and solar mass
together (GM<sub>⊙</sub>) has been determined to much greater precision, the IAU defined the <i>nominal</i> solar mass parameter to be:
</p>
<dl><dd><table>
<tbody><tr>
<td>nominal solar mass parameter:
</td>
<td><i>GM</i><sub>⊙</sub> = <span class="nowrap">1.3271244 × 10<sup>20</sup> m<sup>3</sup> s<sup>−2</sup></span> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prsa16_59-2"><a href="#cite_note-Prsa16-59">[59]</a></sup>
</td></tr></tbody></table></dd></dl>
<p>However, one can combine the nominal solar mass parameter with the most recent (2014) CODATA estimate of the Newtonian gravitational constant G to derive the solar mass to be approximately 1.9885 × 10<sup>30</sup> kg. Although the exact values for the luminosity, radius, mass parameter, and mass may vary slightly in the future due to observational uncertainties, the 2015 IAU nominal constants will remain the same SI values as they remain useful measures for quoting stellar parameters.
</p><p>Large lengths, such as the radius of a giant star or the <a href="/wiki/Semi-major_and_semi-minor_axes" title="Semi-major and semi-minor axes">semi-major axis</a> of a binary star system, are often expressed in terms of the <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">astronomical unit</a>—approximately equal to the mean distance between the Earth and the Sun (150 million km or approximately 93 million miles). In 2012, the IAU defined the <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_constant" title="Astronomical constant">astronomical constant</a> to be an exact length in meters: 149,597,870,700 m.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prsa16_59-3"><a href="#cite_note-Prsa16-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation_and_evolution">Formation and evolution</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Star_life_cycles_red_dwarf_en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="341" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Star_life_cycles_red_dwarf_en.svg/300px-Star_life_cycles_red_dwarf_en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Star_life_cycles_red_dwarf_en.svg/450px-Star_life_cycles_red_dwarf_en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Star_life_cycles_red_dwarf_en.svg/600px-Star_life_cycles_red_dwarf_en.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Star_life_cycles_red_dwarf_en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Stellar evolution of low-mass (left cycle) and high-mass (right cycle) stars, with examples in italics</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Stellar_evolution" title="Stellar evolution">Stellar evolution</a></div>
<p>Stars condense from regions of <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">space</a> of higher matter density, yet those regions are less dense than within a <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_chamber" title="Vacuum chamber">vacuum chamber</a>. These regions—known as <i><a href="/wiki/Molecular_cloud" title="Molecular cloud">molecular clouds</a></i>—consist mostly of hydrogen, with about 23 to 28 percent helium and a few percent heavier elements. One example of such a star-forming region is the <a href="/wiki/Orion_Nebula" title="Orion Nebula">Orion Nebula</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Most stars form in groups of dozens to hundreds of thousands of stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/O-type_star" title="O-type star">Massive stars</a> in these groups may powerfully illuminate those clouds, <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ionizing</a> the hydrogen, and creating <a href="/wiki/H_II_region" title="H II region">H II regions</a>. Such feedback effects, from star formation, may ultimately disrupt the cloud and prevent further star formation.
</p><p>All stars spend the majority of their existence as <i><a href="/wiki/Main_sequence" title="Main sequence">main sequence</a> stars</i>, fueled primarily by the nuclear fusion of hydrogen into helium within their cores. However, stars of different masses have markedly different properties at various stages of their development. The ultimate fate of more massive stars differs from that of less massive stars, as do their luminosities and the impact they have on their environment. Accordingly, astronomers often group stars by their mass:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><i>Very low mass stars</i>, with masses below 0.5 <a href="/wiki/Solar_mass" title="Solar mass"><var>M</var><sub>☉</sub></a>, are fully convective and distribute <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> evenly throughout the whole star while on the main sequence. Therefore, they never undergo shell burning, never become <a href="/wiki/Red_giant" title="Red giant">red giants</a>, which cease fusing and become <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf#Stars_with_very_low_mass" title="White dwarf">helium white dwarfs</a> and slowly cool after exhausting their hydrogen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adams_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-adams-63">[63]</a></sup> However, as the lifetime of 0.5 <var>M</var><sub>☉</sub> stars is longer than the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_the_universe" title="Age of the universe">age of the universe</a>, no such star has yet reached the white dwarf stage.</li>
<li><i>Low mass stars</i> (including the Sun), with a mass between 0.5 <var>M</var><sub>☉</sub> and 1.8–2.5 <var>M</var><sub>☉</sub>  depending on composition, do become red giants as their core hydrogen is depleted and they begin to burn helium in core in a <a href="/wiki/Helium_flash" title="Helium flash">helium flash</a>; they develop a degenerate carbon-oxygen core later on the <a href="/wiki/Asymptotic_giant_branch" title="Asymptotic giant branch">asymptotic giant branch</a>; they finally blow off their outer shell as a <a href="/wiki/Planetary_nebula" title="Planetary nebula">planetary nebula</a> and leave behind their core in the form of a white dwarf.</li>
<li><i>Intermediate-mass stars</i>, between 1.8–2.5 <var>M</var><sub>☉</sub> and 5–10 <var>M</var><sub>☉</sub>, pass through evolutionary stages similar to low mass stars, but after a relatively short period on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red_giant_branch" title="Red giant branch">red giant branch</a> they ignite helium without a flash and spend an extended period in the <a href="/wiki/Red_clump" title="Red clump">red clump</a> before forming a degenerate carbon-oxygen core.</li>
<li><i>Massive stars</i> generally have a minimum mass of 7–10 <var>M</var><sub>☉</sub> (possibly as low as 5–6 <var>M</var><sub>☉</sub>). After exhausting the hydrogen at the core these stars become <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supergiant" title="Supergiant">supergiants</a> and go on to <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">fuse</a> elements heavier than helium. They end their lives when their cores collapse and they explode as supernovae.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Star_formation">Star formation</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Star_formation" title="Star formation">Star formation</a></div>
<p>The formation of a star begins with gravitational instability within a molecular cloud, caused by regions of higher density—often triggered by compression of clouds by radiation from massive stars, expanding bubbles in the interstellar medium, the collision of different molecular clouds, or the <a href="/wiki/Interacting_galaxy" title="Interacting galaxy">collision of galaxies</a> (as in a <a href="/wiki/Starburst_galaxy" title="Starburst galaxy">starburst galaxy</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> When a region reaches a sufficient density of matter to satisfy the criteria for <a href="/wiki/Jeans_instability" title="Jeans instability">Jeans instability</a>, it begins to collapse under its own gravitational force.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Witness_the_Birth_of_a_Star.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2560" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Witness_the_Birth_of_a_Star.jpg/220px-Witness_the_Birth_of_a_Star.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Witness_the_Birth_of_a_Star.jpg/330px-Witness_the_Birth_of_a_Star.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Witness_the_Birth_of_a_Star.jpg/440px-Witness_the_Birth_of_a_Star.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Witness_the_Birth_of_a_Star.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artist's conception of the birth of a star within a dense <a href="/wiki/Molecular_cloud" title="Molecular cloud">molecular cloud</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>As the cloud collapses, individual conglomerations of dense dust and gas form "<a href="/wiki/Bok_globule" title="Bok globule">Bok globules</a>". As a globule collapses and the density increases, the gravitational energy converts into heat and the temperature rises. When the protostellar cloud has approximately reached the stable condition of <a href="/wiki/Hydrostatic_equilibrium" title="Hydrostatic equilibrium">hydrostatic equilibrium</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Protostar" title="Protostar">protostar</a> forms at the core.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> These <a href="/wiki/Pre-main-sequence_star" title="Pre-main-sequence star">pre-main-sequence stars</a> are often surrounded by a <a href="/wiki/Protoplanetary_disk" title="Protoplanetary disk">protoplanetary disk</a> and powered mainly by the conversion of gravitational energy. The period of gravitational contraction lasts about 10 to 15 million years.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:W40_star-forming_region.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1078" data-file-width="1001" decoding="async" height="237" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/W40_star-forming_region.jpg/220px-W40_star-forming_region.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/W40_star-forming_region.jpg/330px-W40_star-forming_region.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/W40_star-forming_region.jpg/440px-W40_star-forming_region.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:W40_star-forming_region.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A cluster of approximately 500 young stars lies within the nearby <a href="/wiki/Westerhout_40" title="Westerhout 40">W40</a> stellar nursery.</div></div></div>
<p>Early stars of less than 2 <var>M</var><sub>☉</sub> are called <a href="/wiki/T_Tauri_star" title="T Tauri star">T Tauri stars</a>, while those with greater mass are <a href="/wiki/Herbig_Ae/Be_star" title="Herbig Ae/Be star">Herbig Ae/Be stars</a>. These newly formed stars emit jets of gas along their axis of rotation, which may reduce the <a href="/wiki/Angular_momentum" title="Angular momentum">angular momentum</a> of the collapsing star and result in small patches of nebulosity known as <a href="/wiki/Herbig%E2%80%93Haro_object" title="Herbig–Haro object">Herbig–Haro objects</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smith04_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-smith04-69">[69]</a></sup>
These jets, in combination with radiation from nearby massive stars, may help to drive away the surrounding cloud from which the star was formed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>Early in their development, T Tauri stars follow the <a href="/wiki/Hayashi_track" title="Hayashi track">Hayashi track</a>—they contract and decrease in luminosity while remaining at roughly the same temperature. Less massive T Tauri stars follow this track to the main sequence, while more massive stars turn onto the <a href="/wiki/Henyey_track" title="Henyey track">Henyey track</a>.
</p><p>Most stars are observed to be members of binary star systems, and the properties of those binaries are the result of the conditions in which they formed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> A gas cloud must lose its angular momentum in order to collapse and form a star. The fragmentation of the cloud into multiple stars distributes some of that angular momentum. The primordial binaries transfer some angular momentum by gravitational interactions during close encounters with other stars in young stellar clusters. These interactions tend to split apart more widely separated (soft) binaries while causing hard binaries to become more tightly bound. This produces the separation of binaries into their two observed populations distributions.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Main_sequence">Main sequence</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Main_sequence" title="Main sequence">Main sequence</a></div>
<p>Stars spend about 90% of their existence fusing hydrogen into helium in high-temperature and high-pressure reactions near the core. Such stars are said to be on the <a href="/wiki/Main_sequence" title="Main sequence">main sequence</a>, and are called dwarf stars. Starting at zero-age main sequence, the proportion of helium in a star's core will steadily increase, the rate of nuclear fusion at the core will slowly increase, as will the star's temperature and luminosity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
The Sun, for example, is estimated to have increased in luminosity by about 40% since it reached the main sequence 4.6 billion (4.6 × 10<sup>9</sup>) years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sun_future_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-sun_future-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>Every star generates a <a href="/wiki/Stellar_wind" title="Stellar wind">stellar wind</a> of particles that causes a continual outflow of gas into space. For most stars, the mass lost is negligible. The Sun loses 10<sup>−14</sup> <var>M</var><sub>☉</sub> every year,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> or about 0.01% of its total mass over its entire lifespan. However, very massive stars can lose 10<sup>−7</sup> to 10<sup>−5</sup> <var>M</var><sub>☉</sub> each year, significantly affecting their evolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Stars that begin with more than 50 <var>M</var><sub>☉</sub> can lose over half their total mass while on the main sequence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:H-R_diagram_-edited-3.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="811" data-file-width="723" decoding="async" height="247" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/H-R_diagram_-edited-3.gif/220px-H-R_diagram_-edited-3.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/H-R_diagram_-edited-3.gif/330px-H-R_diagram_-edited-3.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/H-R_diagram_-edited-3.gif/440px-H-R_diagram_-edited-3.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:H-R_diagram_-edited-3.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An example of a <a href="/wiki/Hertzsprung%E2%80%93Russell_diagram" title="Hertzsprung–Russell diagram">Hertzsprung–Russell diagram</a> for a set of stars that includes the Sun (center). (See "Classification" below.)</div></div></div>
<p>The time a star spends on the main sequence depends primarily on the amount of fuel it has and the rate at which it fuses it. The Sun is expected to live 10 billion (10<sup>10</sup>) years. Massive stars consume their fuel very rapidly and are short-lived. Low mass stars consume their fuel very slowly. Stars less massive than 0.25 <var>M</var><sub>☉</sub>, called <a href="/wiki/Red_dwarf" title="Red dwarf">red dwarfs</a>, are able to fuse nearly all of their mass while stars of about 1 <var>M</var><sub>☉</sub> can only fuse about 10% of their mass. The combination of their slow fuel-consumption and relatively large usable fuel supply allows low mass stars to last about one trillion (10<sup>12</sup>) years; the most extreme of 0.08 <var>M</var><sub>☉</sub>) will last for about 12 trillion years. Red dwarfs become <a href="/wiki/Blue_dwarf_(red-dwarf_stage)" title="Blue dwarf (red-dwarf stage)">hotter and more luminous</a> as they accumulate helium. When they eventually run out of hydrogen, they contract into a white dwarf and decline in temperature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adams_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-adams-63">[63]</a></sup> However, since the lifespan of such stars is greater than the current <a href="/wiki/Age_of_the_universe" title="Age of the universe">age of the universe</a> (13.8 billion years), no stars under about 0.85 <var>M</var><sub>☉</sub><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-saomainseq_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-saomainseq-77">[77]</a></sup> are expected to have moved off the main sequence.
</p><p>Besides mass, the elements heavier than helium can play a significant role in the evolution of stars. Astronomers label all elements heavier than helium "metals", and call the chemical <a href="/wiki/Concentration" title="Concentration">concentration</a> of these elements in a star, its <a href="/wiki/Metallicity" title="Metallicity">metallicity</a>. A star's metallicity can influence the time the star takes to burn its fuel, and controls the formation of its magnetic fields,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> which affects the strength of its stellar wind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Older, <a href="/wiki/Stellar_population" title="Stellar population">population II</a> stars have substantially less metallicity than the younger, population I stars due to the composition of the molecular clouds from which they formed. Over time, such clouds become increasingly enriched in heavier elements as older stars die and shed portions of their <a href="/wiki/Stellar_atmosphere" title="Stellar atmosphere">atmospheres</a>.
</p>
<h3><span id="Post.E2.80.93main_sequence"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Post–main_sequence">Post–main sequence</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Subgiant" title="Subgiant">Subgiant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Red_giant" title="Red giant">Red giant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Horizontal_branch" title="Horizontal branch">Horizontal branch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Red_clump" title="Red clump">Red clump</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Asymptotic_giant_branch" title="Asymptotic giant branch">Asymptotic giant branch</a></div>
<p>As stars of at least 0.4 <var>M</var><sub>☉</sub><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-late_stages_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-late_stages-3">[3]</a></sup> exhaust their supply of hydrogen at their core, they start to fuse hydrogen in a shell outside the helium core. Their outer layers expand and cool greatly as they form a <a href="/wiki/Red_giant" title="Red giant">red giant</a>. In about 5 billion years, when the Sun enters the helium burning phase, it will expand to a maximum radius of roughly 1 <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">astronomical unit</a> (150 million kilometres), 250 times its present size, and lose 30% of its current mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sun_future_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-sun_future-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sun_future_schroder_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-sun_future_schroder-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>As the hydrogen shell burning produces more helium, the core increases in mass and temperature. In a red giant of up to 2.25 <var>M</var><sub>☉</sub>, the mass of the helium core becomes degenerate prior to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Helium_fusion" title="Helium fusion">helium fusion</a>. Finally, when the temperature increases sufficiently, helium fusion begins explosively in what is called a <a href="/wiki/Helium_flash" title="Helium flash">helium flash</a>, and the star rapidly shrinks in radius, increases its surface temperature, and moves to the <a href="/wiki/Horizontal_branch" title="Horizontal branch">horizontal branch</a> of the HR diagram. For more massive stars, helium core fusion starts before the core becomes degenerate, and the star spends some time in the <a href="/wiki/Red_clump" title="Red clump">red clump</a>, slowly burning helium, before the outer convective envelope collapses and the star then moves to the horizontal branch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iben_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-iben-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the star has fused the helium of its core, the carbon product fuses producing a hot core with an outer shell of fusing helium. The star then follows an evolutionary path called the <a href="/wiki/Asymptotic_giant_branch" title="Asymptotic giant branch">asymptotic giant branch</a> (AGB) that parallels the other described red giant phase, but with a higher luminosity. The more massive AGB stars may undergo a brief period of carbon fusion before the core becomes degenerate.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Massive_stars">Massive stars</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Supergiant_star" title="Supergiant star">Supergiant star</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hypergiant" title="Hypergiant">Hypergiant</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wolf%E2%80%93Rayet_star" title="Wolf–Rayet star">Wolf–Rayet star</a></div>
<p>During their helium-burning phase, a star of more than 9 solar masses expands to form first a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blue_supergiant" title="Blue supergiant">blue</a> and then a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red_supergiant" title="Red supergiant">red supergiant</a>. Particularly massive stars may evolve to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wolf-Rayet_star" title="Wolf-Rayet star">Wolf-Rayet star</a>, characterised by spectra dominated by emission lines of elements heavier than hydrogen, which have reached the surface due to strong convection and intense mass loss.
</p><p>When helium is exhausted at the core of a massive star, the core contracts and the temperature and pressure rises enough to fuse <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Carbon-burning_process" title="Carbon-burning process">Carbon-burning process</a>). This process continues, with the successive stages being fueled by <a href="/wiki/Neon" title="Neon">neon</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Neon-burning_process" title="Neon-burning process">neon-burning process</a>), <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Oxygen-burning_process" title="Oxygen-burning process">oxygen-burning process</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Silicon-burning_process" title="Silicon-burning process">silicon-burning process</a>). Near the end of the star's life, fusion continues along a series of onion-layer shells within a massive star. Each shell fuses a different element, with the outermost shell fusing hydrogen; the next shell fusing helium, and so forth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>The final stage occurs when a massive star begins producing <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>. Since iron nuclei are more <a href="/wiki/Binding_energy" title="Binding energy">tightly bound</a> than any heavier nuclei, any fusion beyond iron does not produce a net release of energy. To a very limited degree such a process proceeds, but it consumes energy. Likewise, since they are more tightly bound than all lighter nuclei, such energy cannot be released by <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">fission</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sneden_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-sneden-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Collapse">Collapse</span></h4>
<p>As a star's core shrinks, the intensity of radiation from that surface increases, creating such <a href="/wiki/Radiation_pressure" title="Radiation pressure">radiation pressure</a> on the outer shell of gas that it will push those layers away, forming a <a href="/wiki/Planetary_nebula" title="Planetary nebula">planetary nebula</a>. If what remains after the outer atmosphere has been shed is less than 1.4 <var>M</var><sub>☉</sub>, it shrinks to a relatively tiny object about the size of Earth, known as a <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">white dwarf</a>. White dwarfs lack the mass for further gravitational compression to take place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electron-degenerate_matter" title="Electron-degenerate matter">electron-degenerate matter</a> inside a white dwarf is no longer a plasma, even though stars are generally referred to as being spheres of plasma. Eventually, white dwarfs fade into <a href="/wiki/Black_dwarf" title="Black dwarf">black dwarfs</a> over a very long period of time.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Crab_Nebula.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3864" data-file-width="3864" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Crab_Nebula.jpg/220px-Crab_Nebula.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Crab_Nebula.jpg/330px-Crab_Nebula.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Crab_Nebula.jpg/440px-Crab_Nebula.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Crab_Nebula.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Crab_Nebula" title="Crab Nebula">Crab Nebula</a>, remnants of a supernova that was first observed around 1050 AD</div></div></div>
<p>In massive stars, fusion continues until the iron core has grown so large (more than 1.4 <var>M</var><sub>☉</sub>) that it can no longer support its own mass. This core will suddenly collapse as its electrons are driven into its protons, forming neutrons, neutrinos, and gamma rays in a burst of <a href="/wiki/Electron_capture" title="Electron capture">electron capture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Inverse_beta_decay" title="Inverse beta decay">inverse beta decay</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Shock_wave" title="Shock wave">shockwave</a> formed by this sudden collapse causes the rest of the star to explode in a supernova. Supernovae become so bright that they may briefly outshine the star's entire home galaxy. When they occur within the Milky Way, supernovae have historically been observed by naked-eye observers as "new stars" where none seemingly existed before.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-supernova_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-supernova-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>A supernova explosion blows away the star's outer layers, leaving a <a href="/wiki/Supernova_remnant" title="Supernova remnant">remnant</a> such as the Crab Nebula.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-supernova_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-supernova-84">[84]</a></sup> The core is compressed into a <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star" title="Neutron star">neutron star</a>, which sometimes manifests itself as a <a href="/wiki/Pulsar" title="Pulsar">pulsar</a> or <a href="/wiki/X-ray_burster" title="X-ray burster">X-ray burster</a>. In the case of the largest stars, the remnant is a black hole greater than 4 <var>M</var><sub>☉</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> In a neutron star the matter is in a state known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neutron-degenerate_matter" title="Neutron-degenerate matter">neutron-degenerate matter</a>, with a more exotic form of degenerate matter, <a href="/wiki/QCD_matter" title="QCD matter">QCD matter</a>, possibly present in the core. Within a black hole, the matter is in a state that is not currently understood.
</p><p>The blown-off outer layers of dying stars include heavy elements, which may be recycled during the formation of new stars. These heavy elements allow the formation of rocky planets. The outflow from supernovae and the stellar wind of large stars play an important part in shaping the interstellar medium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-supernova_84-2"><a href="#cite_note-supernova-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Binary_stars">Binary stars</span></h4>
<p>The post–main-sequence evolution of binary stars may be significantly different from the evolution of single stars of the same mass. If stars in a binary system are sufficiently close, when one of the stars expands to become a red giant it may overflow its <a href="/wiki/Roche_lobe" title="Roche lobe">Roche lobe</a>, the region around a star where material is gravitationally bound to that star, leading to transfer of material to the other. When the Roche lobe is violated, a variety of phenomena can result, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Contact_binaries" title="Contact binaries">contact binaries</a>, <a href="/wiki/Common_envelope" title="Common envelope">common-envelope</a> binaries, <a href="/wiki/Cataclysmic_variable_star" title="Cataclysmic variable star">cataclysmic variables</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Type_Ia_supernova" title="Type Ia supernova">type Ia supernovae</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution">Distribution</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sirius_A_and_B_artwork.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Sirius_A_and_B_artwork.jpg/220px-Sirius_A_and_B_artwork.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Sirius_A_and_B_artwork.jpg/330px-Sirius_A_and_B_artwork.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Sirius_A_and_B_artwork.jpg/440px-Sirius_A_and_B_artwork.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sirius_A_and_B_artwork.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">white dwarf</a> star in orbit around <a href="/wiki/Sirius" title="Sirius">Sirius</a> (artist's impression).</div></div></div>
<p>Stars are not spread uniformly across the universe, but are normally grouped into galaxies along with interstellar gas and dust. A typical galaxy contains hundreds of billions of stars, and there are more than 100 billion (10<sup>11</sup>) galaxies in the <a href="/wiki/Observable_universe" title="Observable universe">observable universe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> In 2010, one estimate of the number of stars in the observable universe was 300 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sextillion" title="Sextillion">sextillion</a> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7023300000000000000♠</span>3<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>23</sup></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borenstein2010_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Borenstein2010-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> While it is often believed that stars only exist within galaxies, intergalactic stars have been discovered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Star_system" title="Star system">multi-star system</a> consists of two or more gravitationally bound stars that orbit each other. The simplest and most common multi-star system is a binary star, but systems of three or more stars are also found. For reasons of orbital stability, such multi-star systems are often organized into hierarchical sets of binary stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> Larger groups called star clusters also exist. These range from loose <a href="/wiki/Stellar_kinematics" title="Stellar kinematics">stellar associations</a> with only a few stars, up to enormous <a href="/wiki/Globular_cluster" title="Globular cluster">globular clusters</a> with hundreds of thousands of stars. Such systems orbit their host galaxy.
</p><p>It has been a long-held assumption that the majority of stars occur in gravitationally bound, multiple-star systems. This is particularly true for very massive O and B class stars, where 80% of the stars are believed to be part of multiple-star systems. The proportion of single star systems increases with decreasing star mass, so that only 25% of red dwarfs are known to have stellar companions. As 85% of all stars are red dwarfs, most stars in the Milky Way are likely single from birth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ngc6397_hst_blue_straggler.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="497" data-file-width="625" decoding="async" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Ngc6397_hst_blue_straggler.jpg/220px-Ngc6397_hst_blue_straggler.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Ngc6397_hst_blue_straggler.jpg/330px-Ngc6397_hst_blue_straggler.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Ngc6397_hst_blue_straggler.jpg/440px-Ngc6397_hst_blue_straggler.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ngc6397_hst_blue_straggler.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This view contains blue stars known as "<a href="/wiki/Blue_straggler" title="Blue straggler">Blue stragglers</a>", for their apparent location on the <a href="/wiki/Hertzsprung%E2%80%93Russell_diagram" title="Hertzsprung–Russell diagram">Hertzsprung–Russell diagram</a></div></div></div>
<p>The nearest star to the Earth, apart from the Sun, is <a href="/wiki/Proxima_Centauri" title="Proxima Centauri">Proxima Centauri</a>, which is 39.9 trillion kilometres, or 4.2 light-years. Travelling at the orbital speed of the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a> (8 kilometres per second—almost 30,000 kilometres per hour), it would take about 150,000 years to arrive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> This is typical of stellar separations in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Disc_(galaxy)" title="Disc (galaxy)">galactic discs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> Stars can be much closer to each other in the centres of galaxies and in <a href="/wiki/Globular_cluster" title="Globular cluster">globular clusters</a>, or much farther apart in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galactic_spheroid" title="Galactic spheroid">galactic halos</a>.
</p><p>Due to the relatively vast distances between stars outside the galactic nucleus, collisions between stars are thought to be rare. In denser regions such as the core of globular clusters or the galactic center, collisions can be more common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DarkMatter_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-DarkMatter-93">[93]</a></sup> Such collisions can produce what are known as <a href="/wiki/Blue_straggler" title="Blue straggler">blue stragglers</a>. These abnormal stars have a higher surface temperature than the other main sequence stars with the same luminosity of the cluster to which it belongs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span></h2>
<p>Almost everything about a star is determined by its initial mass, including such characteristics as luminosity, size, evolution, lifespan, and its eventual fate.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Age">Age</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Stellar_age_estimation" title="Stellar age estimation">Stellar age estimation</a></div>
<p>Most stars are between 1 billion and 10 billion years old. Some stars may even be close to 13.8 billion years old—the observed <a href="/wiki/Age_of_the_universe" title="Age of the universe">age of the universe</a>. The oldest star yet discovered, <a href="/wiki/HD_140283" title="HD 140283">HD 140283</a>, nicknamed Methuselah star, is an estimated 14.46 ± 0.8 billion years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bond-140283_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bond-140283-95">[95]</a></sup> (Due to the uncertainty in the value, this age for the star does not conflict with the age of the Universe, determined by the <a href="/wiki/Planck_(spacecraft)" title="Planck (spacecraft)">Planck satellite</a> as 13.799 ± 0.021).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bond-140283_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bond-140283-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Planck_2015_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Planck_2015-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>The more massive the star, the shorter its lifespan, primarily because massive stars have greater pressure on their cores, causing them to burn hydrogen more rapidly. The most massive stars last an average of a few million years, while stars of minimum mass (red dwarfs) burn their fuel very slowly and can last tens to hundreds of billions of years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p>
